<!--StartFragment-->

What is an SVG you ask? SVG stands for Scalable Vector Graphics, they are vector images that can be scaled to any size without issues of resolution loss. Vector images are similar to JPEG and GIF in that they are image file formats, yet different in that they are written in XML and are tags that render the specified shapes or pictures via mathematically defined paths. Taking a closer look inside the SVG tag you may have noticed a viewbox coded inside the tag. Viewbox is displayed as a viewport which defines the part of the SVG image that is displayed and describes the number of units that is perceived. SVG are supported in most mainstream browsers and can be styled with CSS, but the kicker here is that only certain css properties will work with it…in certain browsers. Another great thing about SVG is that you can even write them from scratch if you would prefer. Or you can just use one of the many SVG creators out there, but be careful should you choose to use a SVG editor as you may have to run it through SVG optimizer as the code my not be the cleanest. Now that you have a little background on what SVG you should be able to follow my discussion of styling SVG with CSS , the different techniques you can use, and SVG animation.

\    Common SVG tags:

```
<circle />
<ellipse />
<line />
<polygon />
<polyline />
<rect />
<path />
<text />


```

 </o:OfficeDocumentSettings>
</xml><!\[endif]-->

	mso-tstyle-colband-size:0;
	mso-style-noshow:yes;

<!--StartFragment-->

Embedding SVG inside you're HTML allows the elements in the SVG image to be styled by CSS.

Using CSS you can effect SVG’s color, size, dimensions and so much more. SVG elements such as paths, circles, and other shapes can be targeted by CSS using the SVG attributes as properties. Keep in mind that any attributes that are defined by SVG will be overwritten because CSS has a higher specificity. Some other key benefits to keep in mind with SVG CSS styling is that attribute-styling can be removed to reduce page weight. And remarkably styling SVG with CSS is not limited aesthetic changes you can also use animate, transform (ex.: hover), and translate properties to beautifully manipulate graphics. Using SVG with CSS you can create captivating interactive background images, icons, and graphics that will keep the visitors of your website mesmerized and engaged

<!--EndFragment-->

```
<rect width="50" height="50" fill="blue" />
```

<!--StartFragment-->

Or you can declare it within a style-attribute, if supported be the element.

<!--EndFragment-->

```
<rect width="50" height="50" style="fill:blue;" />
```

<!--StartFragment-->

However, one drawback to this method is that animate attributes will not work with this method. Using this method it’s important to keep in mind the specificity of presentation attributes and styling SVG with CSS.

<!--EndFragment-->

<!--\[if gte mso 9]><xml>
 <o:OfficeDocumentSettings>
  <o:AllowPNG/>
 </o:OfficeDocumentSettings>
</xml><!\[endif]-->

<!--\[if gte mso 9]><xml>
 <w:WordDocument>
  <w:View>Normal</w:View>
  <w:Zoom>0</w:Zoom>
  <w:TrackMoves/>
  <w:TrackFormatting/>
  <w:PunctuationKerning/>
  <w:ValidateAgainstSchemas/>
  <w:SaveIfXMLInvalid>false</w:SaveIfXMLInvalid>
  <w:IgnoreMixedContent>false</w:IgnoreMixedContent>
  <w:AlwaysShowPlaceholderText>false</w:AlwaysShowPlaceholderText>
  <w:DoNotPromoteQF/>
  <w:LidThemeOther>EN-US</w:LidThemeOther>
  <w:LidThemeAsian>X-NONE</w:LidThemeAsian>
  <w:LidThemeComplexScript>X-NONE</w:LidThemeComplexScript>
  <w:Compatibility>
   <w:BreakWrappedTables/>
   <w:SnapToGridInCell/>
   <w:WrapTextWithPunct/>
   <w:UseAsianBreakRules/>
   <w:DontGrowAutofit/>
   <w:SplitPgBreakAndParaMark/>
   <w:EnableOpenTypeKerning/>
   <w:DontFlipMirrorIndents/>
   <w:OverrideTableStyleHps/>
  </w:Compatibility>
  <m:mathPr>
   <m:mathFont m:val="Cambria Math"/>
   <m:brkBin m:val="before"/>
   <m:brkBinSub m:val="&#45;-"/>
   <m:smallFrac m:val="off"/>
   <m:dispDef/>
   <m:lMargin m:val="0"/>
   <m:rMargin m:val="0"/>
   <m:defJc m:val="centerGroup"/>
   <m:wrapIndent m:val="1440"/>
   <m:intLim m:val="subSup"/>
   <m:naryLim m:val="undOvr"/>
  </m:mathPr></w:WordDocument>
</xml><!\[endif]-->

```
Svg{
width: 50px;
 height: 50px;
}

Would override 

<svg height=”30px” width=”30px”>
</svg>

Dosen’t override 

<svg style= “height: 30px; height: 30px; “> 
</svg>

```

	mso-tstyle-colband-size:0;
	mso-style-noshow:yes;

<!--StartFragment-->

There are plenty of ways to animate using the SVG CSS selector, which targets the entire SVG element and uses the Path element controls how the path is drawn or created. To control how the animation is displayed you must first set a @keyframes at-rule that describes the steps in the animation. Next, you must name the keyframe while defining the way that the keyframe is created along the duration of the animation using a percentage or “from” and “to”. You can add as many keyframes as you want for any singular or multiple elements that need animating. The next step is referencing the keyframes name using the animation name as well as an animation attribute.

<!--\[if gte mso 9]><xml>
 <o:OfficeDocumentSettings>
  <o:AllowPNG/>
 </o:OfficeDocumentSettings>
</xml><!\[endif]-->

<!--\[if gte mso 9]><xml>

<!--StartFragment-->

Specifying the pace of each keyframe over specifying the pace of the animation gives you better control over how each keyframe should be displayed. This allows for truly customizable precise detail-oriented animations.

You don’t have to limit yourself to keyword easing functions you could also use cubic-bezier functions. Cubic Bezier are four points defined on cubic-bezier curve, and in CSS they are fixed points that define the animation along the curve. Cubic-bezier functions can be used with the animation-function-property and the transition-timing-function for better effect!

<!--EndFragment-->

</xml><!\[endif]-->

<!--StartFragment-->

Wow, we went through a lot of information about SVGs! Let’s review. SVG are scalable images rendered by mathematical formulas that do not experience resolution loss at larger sizes. Why use SVG? SVG have great browser support, are accessible, and can add to the interactivity and creative design of a website. They are arguably the best way to add animated images to a website (sorry GIF) with out taxing performance with HTTP requests. And yes, if your fancy you could use Javascript, if you to stifle website performance or unnecessarily complicate simple animations. The best way to get back at it again and create captivating websites is with SVG! 

<!--EndFragment-->

<!--EndFragment-->
